```
What follows works through the narrative on the Nyanga tradition's own terms first. The next section sketches the Banyanga, the forest world, and the layered cosmography in which the epic's action unfolds. The chapter then takes up Mwindo's birth and early career, the Kirimu episode itself (provocation, hunt, killing), Nkuba's lightning-strike and the celestial humiliation, the maturation-through-humiliation reading that the Nyanga tradition's own framing supports, the contrastive-case reading against the chaoskampf typology, and the chapter's typological place and area-specialist framing within the wider book.

=== The Banyanga, the forest world, and the epic's setting

The Nyanga (Banyanga) are a Bantu-speaking population of the eastern Democratic Republic of the Congo, with their historical heartland in the upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested terrain in the Kivu region of the eastern Congo. The setting is a high-rainfall equatorial-forest zone where forest hunting, banana cultivation, and small-scale ironworking and trade are the economic base. The *Mwindo Epic* is one of the principal narrative-and-performance traditions of this community and is, per current African-oral-epic scholarship, the flagship recorded epic of the eastern central-African forest-zone Bantu corpus.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the Nyanga (Banyanga) as a Bantu-speaking population of the eastern Democratic Republic of the Congo with historical heartland in the upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested terrain in the Kivu region of the eastern Congo, on the high-rainfall equatorial-forest zone in which they live, on forest hunting / banana cultivation / small-scale ironworking and trade as the economic base, and on the *Mwindo Epic* as one of the community's principal narrative-and-performance traditions; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the *Mwindo Epic* as the flagship recorded epic of the eastern central-African forest-zone Bantu corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The Nyanga oral-tradition's framing of the *Mwindo Epic* is a connected narrative of the hero's life rather than a primarily cosmogonic narrative. The cosmos and the human world are already in place when the epic begins; the narrative's interest lies in the hero's miraculous birth, his disputes with his kin, his journeys across the human and spirit worlds, his testing by the powers above and below, and his eventual chastened return as a properly-formed chief. The epic's compositional centre is the hero's biographical-and-moral arc rather than the establishment of the cosmos.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the *Mwindo Epic*'s framing within Nyanga oral tradition as a connected narrative of the hero's life rather than a cosmogonic narrative, on the already-in-place cosmographic framing of the epic, on the narrative's compositional centre as the hero's biographical-and-moral arc, and on the moral framing of the chastened-return-as-properly-formed-chief outcome; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the didactic (the hero's maturation through humiliation) and social-moral (reciprocal balance between human and spirit worlds) functions of the narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The epic's principal protagonists can be set out at the start. Mwindo is the miraculously-born hero whose name is conventionally rendered with the *karisi* honorific — *karisi* being the Nyanga designation for the trained praise-and-narrative singer-tradition's title and, by extension, for the proper recitation of the epic itself. Mwindo's father is Shemwindo, the chief of Tubondo, whose hostility to his son's birth precipitates the early action of the epic. Mwindo's paternal aunts — especially Iyangura, the river-spirit aunt — shelter and aid him. Nkuba is the sky-god and lightning-bringer who intervenes against Mwindo at the Kirimu episode and is one of the epic's principal divine figures. Muisa is the underworld lord whose realm Mwindo also visits in the journey-sequence. And Kirimu is the dragon whose killing the chapter's narrative-of-interest takes as its centre.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction and Kirimu episode passages on the epic's principal protagonists — Mwindo as miraculously-born hero (with the *karisi* honorific tradition behind the proper recitation of the epic); Shemwindo as Mwindo's hostile chiefly father at Tubondo whose opposition precipitates the early action; Iyangura as Mwindo's paternal aunt and river-spirit shelterer; Nkuba as sky-god and lightning-bringer who intervenes at the Kirimu episode; Muisa as underworld lord; and Kirimu as the dragon at the chapter's narrative centre; per glossary 'Mwindo', 'Kirimu', 'Nkuba' first_mention_glosses on the principal protagonists.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The Nyanga cosmographic framing within which the epic's action unfolds is a layered world. The human world — the forest-and-banana-grove realm of the Nyanga settlements — is bounded above by the sky-realm of Nkuba and the celestial powers, below by the underworld-realm of Muisa and the chthonic powers, and laterally by the river-and-water realm of Iyangura and the riverine spirits. Mwindo's journeys across these realms in the course of the epic are presented within an already-existing layered cosmography rather than as cosmogonic establishment of the layers themselves.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the Nyanga cosmographic framing of the epic — the human world of the forest-and-banana-grove Nyanga settlements bounded above by Nkuba's sky-realm and the celestial powers, below by Muisa's underworld-realm and the chthonic powers, and laterally by Iyangura's river-and-water realm and the riverine spirits — and on Mwindo's journeys across these realms in the course of the epic as movements within an already-existing layered cosmography rather than as cosmogonic establishment of the layers themselves; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the layered-cosmography framing of the epic's action.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

=== Mwindo's birth, growth, and early feats

Mwindo's birth, in the recorded performance, is presented as miraculous in several distinct registers. He is born already speaking and walking, born from his mother's middle finger after his father has forbidden the birth of any son and ordered the killing of any male child his wives bear, and born already in possession of his characteristic implements — a *conga*-scepter, the flywhisk-and-spirit-implement that is in the epic the hero's principal magical-practical instrument. The multiple-register miraculous birth supplies the epic's opening situation in which the hero is exceptional from his first moment.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, opening passages on Mwindo's miraculous birth — born already speaking and walking, born from his mother's middle finger after his father Shemwindo had forbidden the birth of any son and ordered the killing of any male child his wives bore, and born already in possession of the *conga*-scepter (the flywhisk-and-spirit-implement that is the hero's principal magical-practical instrument throughout the epic); on the multiple-register miraculous birth supplying the epic's opening situation of the hero's exceptionality from his first moment.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Mwindo's early action in the epic is structured around his pursuit of recognition by his hostile chiefly father Shemwindo and around the conflict between father and son. Shemwindo, on learning of his son's birth, attempts to have Mwindo killed by various means — sealing him in a drum and casting him into the river is the most widely cited episode of the early epic — and Mwindo survives each attempt and pursues his father across the human and spirit worlds. The early epic's narrative work is the establishment of the hero's character through the father-son conflict rather than through any combat with a serpentine adversary.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, early passages on the conflict between Mwindo and his father Shemwindo, on Shemwindo's attempts to have Mwindo killed (with the drum-and-river episode the most widely cited of the early-epic episodes), on Mwindo's survival of each attempt, and on his subsequent pursuit of Shemwindo across the human and spirit worlds; on the early epic's narrative work as the establishment of the hero's character through the father-son conflict rather than through combat with a serpentine adversary.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Mwindo's journeys, in the middle sections of the epic, take him through the river-realm — where he is sheltered by Iyangura — through the underworld of Muisa, where he is tested and where he eventually defeats or compels the underworld lord, and through further regions of the spirit-and-forest world. The journey-sequence is a sequence of trials each of which adds to the hero's accumulated power and reputation but each of which also occasions warnings, debts, and obligations that the hero accumulates alongside his power.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, middle-epic passages on Mwindo's journeys through the river-realm (where he is sheltered by his aunt Iyangura), through the underworld of Muisa (where he is tested and where he eventually defeats or compels the underworld lord), and through further regions of the spirit-and-forest world; on the journey-sequence as a sequence of trials each of which adds to the hero's accumulated power and reputation but each of which also occasions warnings, debts, and obligations alongside the power; on the hero's accumulating-power-and-accumulating-obligations pattern as a load-bearing feature of the epic's middle sections.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Mwindo's accumulation of power is checked, in the epic's framing, by the warnings he receives from his aunts and from various other figures along the way. The recorded performance repeatedly registers in formulaic refrains and direct narrative comment that Mwindo's growing power is dangerous, that his hubris will require correction, and that the powers above and below are watching the hero's career with a view to the eventual moment at which the correction will fall — a foreshadowing pattern that the narrative develops across multiple episodes before the Kirimu episode arrives.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, throughout the middle-epic passages on the formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments through which Mwindo's accumulating power is repeatedly registered as dangerous, on the warnings the hero receives from his aunts and from other figures, on the announced expectation that the hubris will require correction, and on the powers above and below being presented as watching the hero's career with a view to the eventual moment of correction; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the foreshadowing-of-correction pattern as a load-bearing feature of the epic's middle sections preparing the Kirimu episode and its consequences.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

By the time the Kirimu episode arrives, in the recorded performance's overall structure, Mwindo has already established his exceptional power, defeated multiple adversaries human and spirit, and accumulated reputation as a hero whom the powers above and below regard with concern. The Kirimu episode is therefore not the establishment of the hero's status but a moment within an already-developed heroic career, and the killing of the dragon is presented within a narrative arc that has been preparing the audience for the consequences of the hero's overreach throughout the preceding sections.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, on the structural placement of the Kirimu episode within the overall epic — arriving after Mwindo has already established his exceptional power, defeated multiple adversaries human and spirit, and accumulated heroic reputation — and on the killing of the dragon as a moment within an already-developed heroic career rather than as the establishment of the hero's status; on the narrative arc's preparation of the audience for the consequences of the hero's overreach across the preceding sections.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

=== The Kirimu episode: provocation, hunt, and killing

Kirimu, the dragon at the chapter's narrative centre, is in the recorded performance a creature of the deep forest with multiple heads — the recorded text gives seven in the principal passages, with formulaic-passage variation registered in the editorial apparatus — a long body, and a fire-and-darkness aura. The dragon dwells away from the human settlements in a forest fastness and is not, in the framing the narrator gives the audience, an adversary of the cosmic order or of the human community as such. Kirimu is rather a powerful spirit-being of the forest world whose existence is part of the already-established Nyanga cosmography rather than a primordial chaos against which the hero must establish order.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on Kirimu as a creature of the deep forest with multiple heads (seven in the principal passages, with formulaic-passage variation registered in the editorial apparatus), a long body, and a fire-and-darkness aura; on the dragon's dwelling in a forest fastness away from the human settlements; on Kirimu's narrative framing as a powerful spirit-being of the forest world whose existence is part of the already-established Nyanga cosmography rather than as a primordial chaos against which the hero must establish order; per glossary 'Kirimu' first_mention_gloss on the Nyanga dragon slain by Mwindo.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The provocation that leads Mwindo to seek out Kirimu is, in the recorded performance, the hero's own initiative rather than any prior depredation by the dragon. Mwindo, having already defeated multiple adversaries and accumulated reputation, conceives the project of hunting Kirimu as a further demonstration of his power. The recorded performance presents the project not as a defensive response to the dragon's threat against the human community but as an unprovoked excursion of the hero's that the surrounding narrative voices register as imprudent.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the provocation that leads Mwindo to seek out Kirimu as the hero's own initiative rather than any prior depredation by the dragon, on Mwindo's conceiving the project of hunting Kirimu as a further demonstration of his power after his earlier accumulated victories, on the project's narrative framing as an unprovoked excursion rather than a defensive response to a dragon-threat against the human community, and on the surrounding narrative voices' registration of the project as imprudent.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Mwindo's hunt of Kirimu, in the recorded performance, is structured by the conventional features of the Nyanga heroic-hunt episode. The hero leaves the human settlements with his *conga*-scepter and his accumulated power, travels into the deep forest, locates the dragon at its forest fastness, engages the dragon in a series of head-by-head exchanges in which the hero's *conga*-scepter and accumulated power overcome the dragon's heads and fire-and-darkness aura, and completes the killing. The head-by-head pattern of the killing is a conventional narrative feature shared with the wider Bantu hero-and-monster repertoire rather than a feature distinctive to the chaoskampf typology.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the structure of Mwindo's hunt of Kirimu — the hero's departure from the human settlements with his *conga*-scepter and accumulated power, his travel into the deep forest, his location of the dragon at its forest fastness, his engagement of the dragon in head-by-head exchanges in which the *conga*-scepter and accumulated power overcome the dragon's heads and fire-and-darkness aura, and the completion of the killing; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the head-by-head pattern of the killing as a conventional Bantu heroic-hunt narrative feature rather than a feature distinctive to the chaoskampf typology.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The killing of Kirimu is, in the recorded performance, completed with no immediate triumphal conclusion. There is no narrative passage in which the killing of the dragon establishes the hero's sovereignty, no passage in which the cosmos is reorganised around the dragon's defeat, no festival celebrating the killing as cosmic-order-establishing. The hero kills the dragon, takes the carcass or pieces of it back toward the human settlements, and the narrative immediately turns to the consequence-passage in which Nkuba's response is registered.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the killing's completion with no immediate triumphal conclusion — no passage in which the killing of the dragon establishes the hero's sovereignty, no passage in which the cosmos is reorganised around the dragon's defeat, no festival celebrating the killing as cosmic-order-establishing — and on the immediate narrative turn from the killing to Nkuba's response-passage; on the structural absence of the order-establishing apparatus that would be expected at this point in a chaoskampf-typology narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The narrator's framing of the killing in the recorded performance is, throughout the episode, registered as transgressive rather than triumphal. The recorded text's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments around the killing register the hero's act as exceeding what was permitted, as a hubristic over-reach beyond the bounds the powers above and below have set, and as a moment for which the hero's accumulated power has prepared him only in the technical sense of capability — not in the moral-and-cosmographic sense of authorisation.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the narrator's framing of the killing as transgressive rather than triumphal — formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments registering the hero's act as exceeding what was permitted, as a hubristic over-reach beyond the bounds the powers above and below have set, and as a moment for which the hero's accumulated power has prepared him only in the technical sense of capability rather than in the moral-and-cosmographic sense of authorisation; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the transgression-not-triumph framing of the dragon-killing within the Nyanga tradition's own moral-didactic register.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The killing's transgressive register, in the Nyanga tradition's own framing, is bound up with the reciprocal-balance reading of the human-and-spirit-world relationship. The dragon is a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium; the hero's killing of it is an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world; and the moral-cosmographic principle the narrator's framing registers is that such an act demands a counter-act from the powers above (Nkuba) restoring the equilibrium that the killing has disturbed.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the killing's transgressive register within the reciprocal-balance reading of the human-and-spirit-world relationship — the dragon as a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium; the hero's killing of it as an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world; the moral-cosmographic principle that such an act demands a counter-act from the powers above restoring the equilibrium that the killing has disturbed; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the social-moral function (reciprocal balance between the human and spirit worlds).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

=== The punishment: Nkuba's lightning and the celestial humiliation

Nkuba, the sky-god and lightning-bringer of the Nyanga cosmography, responds to Mwindo's killing of Kirimu by descending in lightning and seizing the hero out of the human world. The recorded performance presents the punishment as immediate, deliberate, and morally-motivated, with the sky-god acting in the role of the cosmographic-authority that the hero's transgressive killing has provoked, and with the seizing-by-lightning conveying the hero from the forest-and-village landscape upward into Nkuba's celestial realm.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, post-killing passages on Nkuba's response to Mwindo's killing of Kirimu — Nkuba's descent in lightning, his seizing of the hero out of the human world, the punishment's immediate-deliberate-morally-motivated character, and the sky-god's role as cosmographic authority responding to the transgressive killing; on the conveyance by lightning from the forest-and-village landscape upward into Nkuba's celestial realm; per glossary 'Nkuba' first_mention_gloss on Nyanga sky-god who punishes Mwindo for the killing of Kirimu.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

In the celestial realm to which Nkuba conveys him, Mwindo is subjected to a sequence of trials and humiliations. The recorded performance presents the hero as exposed to extreme heat and cold in the celestial environment, as compelled to endure the celestial powers' direct presence in a way that strips him of the heroic confidence with which he had killed Kirimu, and as instructed in explicit terms about the limits of his power and the necessity of recognising the cosmographic authority of the powers above.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, celestial-realm passages on the sequence of trials and humiliations to which Nkuba subjects Mwindo in the celestial realm — exposure to extreme heat and cold in the celestial environment, compulsion to endure the celestial powers' direct presence in a way that strips the hero of his heroic confidence, and explicit instruction about the limits of the hero's power and the necessity of recognising the cosmographic authority of the powers above; on the trials-and-humiliations sequence as the celestial counterpart to the chastening that the human-world narrative has been preparing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The punishment's moral-didactic register is explicit in the recorded performance's framing. The celestial humiliation is presented to the audience not as an arbitrary cruelty of the sky-god but as a structurally-necessary correction of the hero's hubristic over-reach, with the recorded text's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments registering Mwindo's celestial chastening as the moment at which the audience is invited to consider the limits of human power against the cosmographic order. The moral-didactic register of the episode is the chapter's most distinctive feature relative to the chaoskampf typology.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, celestial-realm passages on the moral-didactic register of the punishment — the celestial humiliation as a structurally-necessary correction of the hero's hubristic over-reach rather than as arbitrary cruelty; the recorded text's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments registering Mwindo's celestial chastening as the moment at which the audience is invited to consider the limits of human power against the cosmographic order; the moral-didactic register as the episode's most distinctive feature relative to the Eurasian chaoskampf typology in which the dragon-killing is the order-establishing act rather than the act requiring correction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return to the human world include explicit moral-and-conduct conditions. The hero is required to renounce certain practices, to accept the binding force of the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set, and to commit to a chastened mode of conduct as a properly-formed chief on his return. The conditions are explicit, named, and binding within the recorded performance's framing rather than left implicit or symbolic.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, celestial-realm and return-passage on the conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return to the human world — the hero's required renunciation of certain practices, acceptance of the binding force of the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set, and commitment to a chastened mode of conduct as a properly-formed chief on his return; on the conditions as explicit, named, and binding within the recorded performance's framing rather than left implicit or symbolic.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Mwindo's return from the celestial realm to the human world, in the recorded performance, is conditional and supervised. The hero is restored to the forest-and-village landscape under the express terms Nkuba has imposed, and the narrative explicitly registers the return as a return on the celestial powers' terms rather than as a triumphant homecoming on the hero's own terms. The chastened character of the return is structurally as load-bearing for the episode's resolution as the celestial humiliation itself.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, return-passage on Mwindo's return from the celestial realm to the human world as conditional and supervised — restoration to the forest-and-village landscape under Nkuba's express terms, narrative registration of the return as a return on the celestial powers' terms rather than as a triumphant homecoming on the hero's own terms, and the chastened character of the return as structurally load-bearing for the episode's resolution alongside the celestial humiliation itself.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The post-Kirimu narrative continuation in the wider epic, in the recorded performance, presents the chastened hero as at last in a position to assume the chiefly office for which the early-and-middle epic's father-son conflict has been preparing him. The hero's chiefly succession is presented as conditional on his having undergone the celestial humiliation and on his having accepted the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have imposed, with the moral-developmental shape of the epic's overall arc being the maturation-through-humiliation pattern that the brief identifies as the narrative's didactic kernel.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, post-Kirimu and chiefly-succession passages on the chastened hero's assumption of the chiefly office for which the early-and-middle epic's father-son conflict has been preparing him; on the chiefly succession's narrative framing as conditional on the hero's having undergone the celestial humiliation and on his having accepted the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have imposed; on the moral-developmental shape of the epic's overall arc as the maturation-through-humiliation pattern; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the didactic function (the hero's maturation through humiliation) and the religious-political function (the sky-god's authority over the hero) of the narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

=== The maturation-through-humiliation reading: the tradition's own framing

The Nyanga oral tradition's own framing of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is, per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary and cultural_relevance, *maturation-through-humiliation* rather than *cosmogonic combat*. The narrative is told as the story of an exceptional hero whose accumulated power outruns his moral-and-cosmographic discipline, who commits a transgressive killing in the dragon-hunt, who is corrected by the sky-god, and who returns to the human world as a properly-formed chief by virtue of having undergone the correction. The moral-didactic kernel that the narrative communicates to its hearers is the structural necessity of the correction and the limits of unsanctioned heroic power against the cosmographic order.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary on the inverted moral valence of the dragon-slaying as hubris rather than order-making, and on the hero's required humiliation and re-integration as the narrative's didactic centre, with the cultural_relevance.function entry registering didactic (maturation through humiliation), social-moral (reciprocal balance between human and spirit worlds), and religious-political (sky-god's authority over the hero) functions; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, throughout the Kirimu episode and following passages, on the maturation-through-humiliation framing as the Nyanga tradition's own narrative-structural register; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the moral-didactic kernel and on the limits-of-heroic-power register of the episode within the Nyanga tradition's own framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The narrative's moral-didactic register within the Nyanga tradition is therefore directed at the audience of the *karisi* performance rather than at any cosmogonic question. The singer's audience is invited to recognise in Mwindo a hero of exceptional gifts whose very gifts produce his transgression, to feel the gravity of the moment at which Nkuba's lightning falls, and to take the celestial humiliation and the chastened return as a moral-cosmographic teaching about the limits of human power against the powers above and below. The moral-didactic function and the social-moral function — reciprocal balance between the human and spirit worlds — are intertwined within the narrative's tradition-internal performance context.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, throughout the Kirimu episode and following passages, on the moral-didactic register of the narrative within the Nyanga tradition as directed at the audience of the *karisi* performance rather than at any cosmogonic question, on the audience being invited to recognise in Mwindo a hero of exceptional gifts whose very gifts produce his transgression, on the gravity of the moment at which Nkuba's lightning falls, and on the celestial humiliation and chastened return as moral-cosmographic teaching about the limits of human power against the powers above and below; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the intertwined didactic and social-moral functions within the narrative's tradition-internal performance context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The religious-political register of the episode within the Nyanga tradition is the sky-god Nkuba's authority over the hero. The recorded performance presents Mwindo's transgression and its correction in a register that is also a register about chiefly authority: the proper chief is the chief who has undergone the correction at the hands of the celestial powers, and the chiefly office is one whose legitimacy is established not by triumph over a draconic adversary but by the chief's having submitted to the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, post-Kirimu and chiefly-succession passages on the religious-political register of the episode as the sky-god Nkuba's authority over the hero — the recorded performance's presentation of Mwindo's transgression and correction in a register that is also a register about chiefly authority; the proper chief as the chief who has undergone the correction at the hands of the celestial powers; the chiefly office as one whose legitimacy is established not by triumph over a draconic adversary but by the chief's having submitted to the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the religious-political function of the narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Comparable maturation-through-humiliation narrative patterns are attested across other African oral-epic traditions — the Mande Sunjata corpus's treatment of the hero's exile, infirmity, and chastened return; the Bambana hunters' epic tradition's treatment of the hero's ritual obligations and the moral consequences of breach; the Fang *mvet* tradition's treatment of the hero's humiliation by the spirit-world powers. The chapter's task is to register the existence of these comparable patterns without assimilating them into a single comparative category and without assimilating them into the Eurasian chaoskampf category, in line with the brief's cross_cultural_parallels guidance and with scope.md §"Appropriation protocol".footnote:[Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo and chs. on related African oral-epic traditions, on comparable maturation-through-humiliation narrative patterns attested across other African oral-epic traditions — the Mande Sunjata corpus's treatment of the hero's exile, infirmity, and chastened return; the Bambana hunters' epic tradition's treatment of the hero's ritual obligations and the moral consequences of breach; the Fang *mvet* tradition's treatment of the hero's humiliation by the spirit-world powers — and on the chapter's task of registering these comparable patterns without assimilating them into a single comparative category and without assimilating them into the chaoskampf category; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the African material's distinctive non-mapping onto comparativist expectations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

=== The contrastive case: refusal of the chaoskampf valence

Only at this point, with the Nyanga tradition's own framing established, does the chapter turn to the comparative chaoskampf category. The reading developed in this section is itself a comparativist-overlay synthesis drawn from the brief, from Biebuyck & Mateene 1969's translators' apparatus, from Belcher 1999's comparative African-oral-epic treatment, and from scope.md's CONTRASTIVE / Inversion catalog entry; it is flagged as such before the body prose develops the contrastive claim:

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode as the present book's clearest *contrastive case* to the chaoskampf typology — in which the moral valence of the dragon-slaying is inverted (hubris rather than order-making), the killing is followed by the hero's punishment by the celestial authority rather than by the establishment of cosmic order or sovereignty, and the narrative's own moral-didactic frame explicitly rejects the chaos-order mapping that the chaoskampf typology presupposes — is a synthesis of the brief's framing per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary and special_instructions, of Biebuyck & Mateene 1969's translators' apparatus on the Nyanga tradition's own framing, of Belcher 1999's comparative African-oral-epic treatment, and of scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion entry on Mwindo as a contrastive case alongside Bida (chapter 28) and Nanabozho (chapter 32); the contrastive reading is held in the chapter as the chapter's principal load-bearing comparative reading and as the present book's clearest non-Eurasian case in which the tradition's own framing explicitly refuses the chaoskampf typology's moral valence. | basis: brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary explicitly registers the inverted-moral-valence-as-hubris-rather-than-order-making feature that distinguishes the Mwindo / Kirimu episode from the Eurasian chaoskampf material; brief special_instructions classifies the chapter as "the clearest case in the book of a tradition whose own framing explicitly rejects the chaos-order mapping" and instructs that the chapter is to feed Part II C7 (inversion) and especially C8 (refusal and appropriation); scope.md §"In-scope catalog" lists Mwindo as a contrastive case; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969's translators' apparatus and the recorded performance's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments develop the killing-as-transgression framing throughout the Kirimu episode and the punishment-and-celestial-humiliation continuation. | risk: the contrastive reading is itself a 20th-century-Western comparativist overlay on the Nyanga tradition's own framing of the narrative as maturation-through-humiliation (per the preceding section); the chapter's task is to hold the contrastive reading as a load-bearing comparative reading while explicitly registering that the tradition's own framing does not anticipate it, and that the chaoskampf typology against which the reading registers the contrast is itself an external category whose application to the Nyanga material requires the area-specialist objection to typological-assimilation that the [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker below registers — per brief special_instructions on "the resistance is the data" and per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3 on opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative category and on holding the comparative category loosely where the tradition's own framing does not anticipate it.]

The contrastive-case framing of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode against the chaoskampf typology operates at the level of the narrative's *moral valence* rather than at the level of its *structural pattern*. Where the Eurasian chaoskampf adversaries (Tiamat, Vṛtra, Apep, Lôtan, Aži Dahāka, Jörmungandr, Typhon) are adversaries of the cosmos or of the hero's people whose defeat establishes the cosmic order or the hero's sovereignty, Kirimu is a spirit-being of the forest world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium. Where the Eurasian dragon-killing is the act that establishes order, the killing of Kirimu is the act that disturbs order. Where the Eurasian hero is celebrated for the killing, Mwindo is punished for it.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four chaoskampf order-making registers (cosmogonic, sovereignty, cyclical-maintenance, eschatological); per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion on Mwindo as a contrastive case; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 throughout the Kirimu episode and following passages on the moral-valence inversion at the levels of the dragon's narrative status (cosmographic-equilibrium spirit-being rather than adversary of cosmos or hero's people), of the killing's narrative outcome (disturbance of order rather than establishment of order), and of the hero's narrative reception (punishment rather than celebration).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The contrastive-case status of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode operates more strongly than the inversion-case status of the Bida / Wagadu narrative of chapter 28 in one important respect. Where the Bida narrative's moral framing is covenant-and-consequence rather than chaos-versus-order, the killing of Bida is at least within the narrative a question of human moral responsibility (Mamadi Sefe Dekote's choice between his beloved and the city's prosperity), with no celestial power explicitly intervening to punish the killer. In the Mwindo / Kirimu episode the celestial power Nkuba is explicit, named, and active as the punisher of the hero's killing, with the consequence that the chaoskampf-typology refusal is registered within the narrative itself rather than only at the level of the narrative's outcome.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, throughout the Kirimu episode and following passages, on Nkuba as explicit, named, and active celestial-authority punisher of the hero's killing within the narrative itself; on the contrast with the Bida / Wagadu narrative of chapter 28 in which the killing's consequence (the abandonment of the polity and the dispersal of the Soninke) is registered at the level of the narrative's outcome but no celestial power is explicit, named, and active as the punisher of the killer within the narrative itself; on the consequence that the chaoskampf-typology refusal in the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is registered within the narrative rather than only at the level of the narrative's outcome.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The contrastive-case status of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode operates differently from the related-tier transformation cases of the present book — Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi of chapter 23 read as sword-etiology and metallurgical allegory; Pariacaca and the amaru of chapter 26 read as wak'a-succession — and from the related-tier inversion case of chapter 28 (Bida / Wagadu). The transformation cases hold the narrative's combat structure but transform the chaoskampf interpretation of what the combat means; the inversion case (Bida) holds the structural pattern of the chaoskampf combat but reverses the order-establishing valence at every load-bearing point; the contrastive case (Mwindo / Kirimu) holds the surface-level dragon-killing event but the narrative's own moral-didactic frame refuses the chaoskampf typology by punishing rather than celebrating the killer — the strongest of the three forms of resistance.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion / transformation problem and on the distinction between transformation cases (chaoskampf interpretation transforms when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing) and inversion cases (chaoskampf structural pattern present with valences reversed); per the brief 23-susanoo-orochi.yaml on the metallurgical-allegory transformation case; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml on the wak'a-succession transformation case; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the slaying-ends-prosperity inversion case; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml on the killing-as-hubris-and-hero-punished contrastive case as the strongest of the three forms of resistance.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The contrastive reading must, however, be held as a *comparativist* reading rather than as a tradition-internal structural feature. The Nyanga tradition's own framing of the episode is maturation-through-humiliation (per the preceding section), the contrastive-case-against-chaoskampf reading is a 20th-century-Western comparativist overlay, and the chapter's load-bearing methodological discipline is to hold the comparativist contrastive frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation. The heuristic's value is precisely that it can register what the Eurasian chaoskampf pattern's structural-and-moral assumptions look like when held against a structurally-similar narrative whose own moral framing refuses them, while remaining a 20th-century-Western reading of the narrative rather than a Nyanga reading.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary on the inverted moral valence and on the resistance-as-data framing; per brief special_instructions on "the resistance is the data" and on the chapter's contribution to Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation); per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3 on opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative category and on holding the comparative category loosely where the tradition's own framing does not anticipate it; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 throughout on the Nyanga tradition's own maturation-through-humiliation framing as the load-bearing tradition-internal register against which the comparativist contrastive frame is held.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

A structural parallel that the brief explicitly registers — though it is not a comparativist assimilation — is the Krishna / Kāliya episode of chapter 15 of the present book, in which Krishna *subdues* but does not *kill* the serpent Kāliya, with the bhakti-tradition's framing of the episode being a moral transformation of the Vedic Indra / Vṛtra dragon-killing pattern within the same tradition (Indra-as-precursor). The Krishna / Kāliya parallel is structurally analogous to the Mwindo / Kirimu episode in registering an explicit moral discipline against unrestrained dragon-killing, but is unlike the Mwindo / Kirimu episode in being a within-tradition transformation of the chaoskampf pattern (Vedic to bhakti) rather than a non-Eurasian-tradition contrastive case in which the chaoskampf typology was never tradition-internal to begin with.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels explicitly registering the Krishna / Kāliya parallel as structurally analogous to the Mwindo / Kirimu episode in registering an explicit moral discipline against unrestrained dragon-killing, with the qualification that the Krishna case is a moral transformation within the same Vedic-and-post-Vedic tradition (Indra-as-precursor) whereas the Mwindo case is a different tradition entirely; per the chapter-15 (Vedic reflexes) brief on the Krishna / Kāliya episode's bhakti-tradition framing as moral transformation of the Vedic Indra / Vṛtra dragon-killing pattern within the same tradition; per the structural-vs-genetic distinction between within-tradition transformation (Krishna / Kāliya) and non-Eurasian-tradition contrastive case (Mwindo / Kirimu) registered at the present claim.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

=== Typology, area-specialist framing, and forward-links

The contrastive reading developed in the preceding section is the specific outcome of an area-specialist objection to the typological assimilation of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode to the Eurasian chaoskampf category, and the chapter flags the resulting appropriation issue explicitly under the marker the project reserves for cases of this kind:

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: Nyanga-area / Bantu-and-African-oral-epic-studies area specialists — including Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene as the principal field-and-text scholars of the Nyanga *Mwindo Epic* across the *Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga* of 1969 and Biebuyck's later Nyanga epic publications including *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga* of 1978; Stephen Belcher in *Epic Traditions of Africa* of 1999 and his wider corpus of African-oral-epic scholarship; Thomas A. Hale in *Griots and Griottes* of 1998 and his work on African oral-tradition performance more broadly; the wider African-oral-epic comparative-scholarly tradition including the work of Pius Ngandu Nkashama on Bantu and Congolese oral epics, of Mugyabuso M. Mulokozi and the Tanzanian Bahaya / Banyoro epic-recording tradition, and of Charles S. Bird's pioneering Mande oral-epic scholarship — read the Mwindo / Kirimu episode within the registers of Nyanga oral-and-performance tradition, of the Bantu and African forest-zone oral-epic comparative apparatus, and of the maturation-through-humiliation moral-didactic register that the Nyanga *karisi* performance tradition supports, rather than within the chaoskampf-typology register imported from Gunkel-tradition combat-myth scholarship. The area-specialist objection to the chaoskampf typology's application to the Mwindo material does not, however, take the form of rejecting the dragon-killing event as such (the dragon-killing is unambiguously present in the recorded performance) or of rejecting the cross-cultural-comparison framing entirely (Belcher 1999's comparative apparatus places the Mwindo material in a wider African oral-epic comparison): the area-specialist objection is rather to the *typological assimilation* of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode to the Eurasian chaoskampf category — to which the episode's moral valence is inverted at the load-bearing point of the killing-and-its-aftermath, with the celestial-authority-punishing-the-killer narrative form being the sharpest possible refusal of the chaoskampf moral assumption that the dragon-killer is the order-establisher. Position on the spectrum of objection-strengths: the Nyanga-area objection is structurally similar to the Mande-area objection registered in chapter 28 (Bida) — area-specialist objection to typological-assimilation rather than to combat-myth-recognition as such — but operates more strongly than the Bida case in that the Nyanga tradition's own framing explicitly punishes rather than ambiguously-pays-the-cost-of the killing, with the celestial-authority-as-punisher narrative form being the most explicit possible tradition-internal repudiation of the chaoskampf moral valence; the Nyanga-area objection is on the spectrum of objection-strengths between the Mande-area objection in Ch28 (covenant-and-consequence framing whose convergence with the inversion frame is structural rather than explicit) and the Anishinaabe-area Hallowell-Johnston-T. Smith-Vizenor objection in Ch32 (necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing in which combat-and-defeat-of-the-other is rejected as such), with the Nyanga-area objection sharing the typological-assimilation-rather-than-combat-recognition character of the Mande-area objection but registering the moral-valence refusal explicitly within the narrative as the Anishinaabe-area objection rejects the combat framing structurally. The chapter's load-bearing methodological move is therefore to use the contrastive-case reading as the heuristic that registers exactly this objection: the dragon-killing is recognised (the area-specialist framing accommodates this), but the typology of order-establishment that the chaoskampf category presupposes is registered as explicitly refused in the Mwindo / Kirimu case (the area-specialist framing of maturation-through-humiliation aligns with the chapter's contrastive-case frame on this structural-and-moral question, with the Nyanga *karisi* tradition's own register and the chapter's comparative register reaching the same conclusion by different routes — the tradition's register being the moral-didactic-and-cosmographic register and the chapter's register being the comparativist-typological register). Forwarded to Part II C7 (inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40 of the present volume) as a contrastive-tier worked instance of the moral-valence-refusal sub-problem within the wider inversion / transformation problem complex, paired with the Bida case (Ch28) as the Africa-cluster co-comparandum and contrasted with the within-tradition Krishna-Kāliya transformation case (Ch15). Forwarded to Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation problem; ch. 41) as the BOOK'S CLEAREST CASE of the refusal sub-problem proper — per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's load-bearing role for C8 — alongside the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material of chapter 32 (the Anishinaabe-area Hallowell-Johnston-T. Smith-Vizenor refusal case in the strong sense of refusing the combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing as such) and the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks). Marker placement: structural marker at the opening of the typology / forward-links section, immediately after the contrastive-case section's closing claim, per the appropriation-flag-at-comparative-section placement convention of the present book's non-Eurasian related-tier and contrastive chapters. Biebuyck and Mateene are the chapter's principal anchors and named in the first factual pass per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions and per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 1; Belcher is named in the source-overview and in the comparative-apparatus claims; Hale, Ngandu Nkashama, Mulokozi, and Bird are named in body prose at this marker per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (Hale's *Griots and Griottes*, Indiana University Press 1998 — also named at the Ch28 [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker and not currently in sources.yaml; Pius Ngandu Nkashama's Bantu-and-Congolese oral-epic publications; Mugyabuso M. Mulokozi's Tanzanian Bahaya / Banyoro epic-recording publications; Charles Bird's Mande oral-epic publications — none currently in sources.yaml; deferred to inventory-audit per Ch23 FC-02, Ch26 FC-02, Ch27 source-pinning, and Ch28 FC-02 named-but-flagged precedents). Biebuyck's later *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga* of 1978 is also named in body prose only and is not currently a sources.yaml entry; deferred to inventory-audit alongside the secondary specialists.]

The Mwindo / Kirimu episode's combat-and-killing structural pattern is recognisable across the recorded performance: a hero (Mwindo) confronts a serpentine-and-multi-headed adversary (the seven-headed Kirimu) in a defined combat-encounter (at the dragon's forest fastness), wields an implement (the *conga*-scepter and his accumulated power) against the dragon's heads in a head-by-head pattern of exchanges, and successfully completes the killing. The combat-pattern has every surface-level feature of a hero-slay-serpent narrative, and the contrastive-case status operates at the level of *what the killing is morally and cosmographically* rather than at the level of *what the killing is at the surface narrative level*.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the combat-and-killing structural pattern of the episode as recognisable across the recorded performance — hero confronting serpentine-and-multi-headed adversary in a defined combat-encounter at the dragon's forest fastness, wielding the *conga*-scepter and his accumulated power against the dragon's heads in a head-by-head pattern of exchanges, and completing the killing — and on the contrastive-case status of the episode operating at the level of what the killing is morally and cosmographically (transgression to be punished by the celestial authority) rather than at the level of what the killing is at the surface narrative level (a hero-slay-serpent encounter in the standard register).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C8 (the comparative chapter on the refusal and the appropriation problem, ch. 41 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes the *book's clearest case* of the refusal sub-problem proper: a story in which the chaoskampf category's surface combat-and-killing pattern is present but in which the tradition's own moral-and-cosmographic framing explicitly refuses the typology's order-establishing valence by punishing rather than celebrating the killer, with the chapter's task being to hold the contrastive-case frame as the heuristic that registers exactly this refusal while also flagging that the contrastive frame is itself a 20th-century-Western comparativist overlay on the Nyanga tradition's own maturation-through-humiliation framing.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation) as especially load-bearing — "The chapter feeds Part II C7 (inversion) and especially C8 (refusal and appropriation)" — and on the chapter being "the clearest case in the book of a tradition whose own framing explicitly rejects the chaos-order mapping"; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C8 on the refusal-and-appropriation comparative chapter; per the chapter's status as the present book's clearest worked instance of the refusal sub-problem proper, distinguished from the Bida-case inversion sub-problem of chapter 28 and from the Anishinaabe Nanabozho / Mishipeshu refusal case of chapter 32 by the explicit-celestial-authority-punishing-the-killer narrative form within the recorded text itself.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to Part II C7 (the comparative chapter on the inversion / transformation problem, ch. 40 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes a contrastive-tier worked instance of the moral-valence-refusal sub-problem within the wider inversion / transformation problem complex, paired with the Bida case (Ch28) as the Africa-cluster co-comparandum and contrasted with the within-tradition Krishna-Kāliya transformation case (Ch15). The Nyanga-area-specialist tradition (Biebuyck, Mateene, Belcher, Hale, Ngandu Nkashama, Mulokozi, Bird) reads the narrative within the registers of Nyanga oral-and-performance tradition and Bantu-and-African-oral-epic comparison rather than within the chaoskampf-typology register, and the chapter's appropriation-protocol-compliant treatment is to use the contrastive-case frame as the heuristic that registers the area-specialist objection rather than as a typology that imposes the Eurasian pattern on the Nyanga material.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy (Biebuyck and Mateene named in the first factual pass; the wider Nyanga / Bantu / African-oral-epic-studies tradition acknowledged); per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement and on the comparative chapter's task of addressing area-specialist objections rather than papering over them; per the chapter's [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker registering the Nyanga-area objection to typological-assimilation; per the spectrum-of-objection-strengths positioning between the Mande-area Bida-case objection (Ch28) and the Anishinaabe-area Nanabozho-case objection (Ch32).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

The chapter's contribution to the methods sidebar (chapter 33 of the present volume, between Part I and Part II) is on three methodological points. First, the single-recorded-performance situation: the Mwindo material is a 1956 single-field-encounter recording rather than a deep multi-recording ethnographic corpus across decades, which the chapter handles via the [LACUNA] marker on pre-modern attestations and via continuous methodological registration of the colonial-ethnographic-mediation in the form of the late-Belgian-colonial-period field-encounter context. Second, the contrastive-case methodological move: the use of the comparativist contrastive-case-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation, which the chapter handles via the [INFERENCE] marker on the contrastive reading and via the explicit comparativist-overlay registration in body prose. Third, the area-specialist citation policy as applied to non-Eurasian non-textual traditions, which the chapter handles via the [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker and via the named-but-flagged secondary-specialists pattern for Hale, Ngandu Nkashama, Mulokozi, and Bird alongside the Biebuyck-Mateene and Belcher anchors.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy and on the oral-performance recording-history situation of Shé-Kárisi Rureke as named 1956 narrator; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement, and on §"Reconstruction policy" point 1 on no cross-tradition reconstruction; chapter 33 methods sidebar (per toc.yaml) as the chapter that articulates these methodological points at full length; the chapter's status as the present book's clearest worked instance of the refusal-of-the-chaoskampf-typology methodological move makes it a central reference point for the methods sidebar's discussion of comparativist heuristics held at appropriate distance from area-specialist consensus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to chapter 28 on Bida and the founding of Wagadu, with which the present chapter shares the African-cluster classification and the area-specialist-objection registration pattern, but contrasts in the substance of the area-specialist objection. The Bida narrative is the related-tier *inversion* case in which the chaoskampf category's structural features are present but with their valences reversed at every load-bearing point, while the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the contrastive-case-tier *refusal* case in which the surface combat-pattern is present but the narrative's own moral framing explicitly refuses the chaoskampf typology by punishing rather than celebrating the killer. The two African-cluster chapters between them constitute (per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels) the present book's strongest "the pattern doesn't go where comparativists expect" cluster, and anchor different sub-problems within Part II C7 and C8 from a single geographic-cluster region.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 and C8 on the inversion / transformation problem and the refusal / appropriation problem; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the related-tier (full, inversion anchor) classification of the Bida narrative and on its contribution to Part II C7 as the principal load-bearing test case for the inversion sub-problem; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml on the contrastive-case-tier (full) classification of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode and on its contribution to Part II C8 as the book's clearest case for the refusal sub-problem, with cross_cultural_parallels explicitly registering the Bida-and-Mwindo pairing as the African-cluster "pattern doesn't go where comparativists expect" cluster of the present book.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's secondary back-links within the present book are to chapter 15 on Vedic reflexes — specifically the Krishna / Kāliya episode of the bhakti-tradition reworking of the Vedic Indra / Vṛtra material — a within-tradition-transformation parallel to the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's punishment-of-the-dragon-killer pattern, registered in the brief's cross_cultural_parallels as a structural analogue with the qualification that the Krishna case is a moral transformation within the same Vedic-and-post-Vedic tradition while the Mwindo case is a different tradition entirely; to chapter 24 (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec) on the Americas-cluster paradigmatic-establishment-of-sovereignty contrast, with the Mwindo / Kirimu episode being the contrastive case at the opposite typological pole within the non-Eurasian comparison; and to chapter 26 (Pariacaca and the amaru) and chapter 27 (Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren) as further non-Eurasian-cluster co-comparanda for the wider non-Eurasian-cluster appropriation-protocol concerns of the present book — with the Mapuche back-link registered without consultation of the chapter-27 material per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement governing the present chapter's relation to the parallel-running chapter-30 (Bakunawa) work.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 6 (India), cluster 10 (Americas), and cluster 11 (Africa) on the chapter ordering of 15 (Vedic reflexes including Krishna / Kāliya), 24 (Huitzilopochtli core-tier), 25 (Mesoamerican family related-tier), 26 (Pariacaca related-tier), 27 (Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren related-family), 28 (Bida related-tier inversion anchor), 29 (Mwindo contrastive-case full); per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels explicitly registering the Krishna / Kāliya structural-analogue with the within-tradition-transformation qualification; per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement that the present chapter not consult the chapter-30 (Bakunawa) parallel-running material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The cultural-historical importance of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode within the Nyanga tradition and within current African-oral-epic scholarship is significant. The *Mwindo Epic* is one of the principal narrative-and-performance traditions of the Nyanga community, the 1956 Rureke recording is one of the flagship texts of the African-oral-epic comparative-scholarly tradition since the publication of the Biebuyck-Mateene 1969 critical edition, and the episode's place within the wider Bantu and African oral-epic tradition (alongside the Mande Sunjata corpus, the Bambana hunters' epic tradition, the Wolof Kajoor cycle, and the Fang *mvet* tradition) is itself one of the load-bearing features of current African-oral-epic scholarship.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo and chs. on related African oral-epic traditions, on the *Mwindo Epic* as one of the principal narrative-and-performance traditions of the Nyanga community, on the 1956 Rureke recording as one of the flagship texts of the African-oral-epic comparative-scholarly tradition since the publication of the Biebuyck-Mateene 1969 critical edition, and on the episode's place within the wider Bantu and African oral-epic tradition alongside the Mande Sunjata corpus, the Bambana hunters' epic tradition, the Wolof Kajoor cycle, and the Fang *mvet* tradition; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cultural_relevance.centrality on the centrality of the Mwindo material within Nyanga oral-epic tradition and on its significance as type-case in African epic studies.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

The chapter closes by registering its load-bearing methodological function for the present book as a whole. The Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the present book's clearest worked instance of the question of whether the chaoskampf typology, when held as a Western-comparativist heuristic against a non-Eurasian narrative whose own moral-and-cosmographic framing explicitly refuses the typology's order-establishing valence, illuminates the structural-and-moral assumptions of the typology itself or whether it instead obscures the load-bearing features of the non-Eurasian narrative — a question that Part II C8 (ch. 41) is to investigate at full length and for which the present chapter supplies the BOOK'S CLEAREST WORKED INSTANCE per brief special_instructions, the resistance to the typology being not an obstacle to the chapter's work but the chapter's data.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter being "the clearest case in the book of a tradition whose own framing explicitly rejects the chaos-order mapping" and on the chapter's special-load-bearing role for Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation), with the special_instructions clause "the resistance is the data" governing the chapter's treatment; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C8 on the refusal-and-appropriation problem comparative chapter (ch. 41 of the present volume); per the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for the present book as the book's clearest worked instance of the refusal-of-the-chaoskampf-typology methodological move.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — direct moral-valence contrast for the Contrastive Case: Marduk's killing of Tiamat ESTABLISHES the cosmos and Marduk's sovereignty over the assembled gods, with the *Enūma Eliš* presenting the killing as the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty-establishing act par excellence and the killer as the celebrated and elevated hero of the divine assembly; Mwindo's killing of Kirimu is followed by Mwindo's PUNISHMENT by the sky-god Nkuba, with the recorded Nyanga performance presenting the killing as a hubristic transgression that the celestial authority must correct and the killer as the chastened hero of the maturation-through-humiliation arc — the same surface combat-and-killing structural feature with the moral valence not merely missing but explicitly inverted by the celestial-authority-as-punisher narrative form. The Marduk-Mwindo contrast is the present book's central worked instance of the contrastive-case framing at the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty level and is registered as load-bearing for Part II C7 and especially C8.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Anatolia and Levant (Baal-Yamm, Ch. 7) — combat-myth typological contrast at the sovereignty-establishment level: Baal's defeat of Yamm establishes Baal's sovereignty over the assembled gods and the cosmic-order distribution of authority, with the *Baal Cycle*'s palace-construction sequence following from the combat-victory; the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the contrastive case in which the combat-and-killing of the serpentine adversary is followed not by the establishment of the hero's sovereignty but by the celestial-authority's intervention against the killer — with Mwindo's chiefly succession's narrative legitimacy depending on his having undergone the celestial humiliation rather than on his having defeated the dragon. The Baal-Mwindo contrast is registered for Part II C8 alongside the Marduk-Mwindo contrast as the principal worked instance of the moral-valence-refusal at the sovereignty-establishment level.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: India (Indra-Vṛtra, Ch. 14) — Vedic-tradition direct-precedent contrast: Indra's killing of Vṛtra is the paradigmatic Vedic dragon-killing as cosmogonic-and-sovereignty-establishing act, with the post-killing celebration of Indra by the gods being the standard register of the act's reception; Mwindo's killing of Kirimu inverts the post-killing reception entirely, with Nkuba's lightning-and-celestial-humiliation taking the place of the celebratory reception — the Indra-Vṛtra precedent supplying the chaoskampf-typology valence against which the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's contrastive-case status is registered.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: India (Krishna-Kāliya, Ch. 15) — within-tradition-transformation parallel to the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's punishment-of-the-dragon-killer pattern: the bhakti-tradition's reworking of the Vedic Indra / Vṛtra dragon-killing pattern via Krishna's *subduing-not-killing* of Kāliya is structurally analogous to the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's *killing-but-then-punishing* pattern in registering an explicit moral discipline against unrestrained dragon-killing, with the qualification (per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels) that the Krishna case is a moral transformation within the same Vedic-and-post-Vedic tradition (Indra-as-precursor) while the Mwindo case is a different tradition entirely. The Krishna-Mwindo pairing is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the within-tradition-transformation versus across-tradition-contrastive-case distinction.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Egypt (Ra-Apep, Ch. 16) — cyclical-maintenance-register contrast: the daily Apep-overthrowing ritual of the Egyptian solar-cycle is the *cyclical-maintenance* practice in the strong sense, in which the killing-of-the-chaos-bringer is *re-enacted* daily as the means of maintaining the cosmic order with Ra as the order-maintainer; the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is structurally a *non-cyclical* combat (a single dragon-killing episode within the hero's biographical-and-moral arc) but registers the same moral asymmetry between the hero's act and the cosmographic order as the Apep-Ra material registers between the daily-overthrowing and the cosmic-order, with the asymmetry inverted in the Mwindo case (the hero's act disturbs rather than maintains the cosmographic order). The Apep-Mwindo contrast is registered for Part II C7 and C8 as a worked instance of the order-maintenance-versus-order-disturbance distinction.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Americas (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — order-establishment contrast at the paradigmatic non-Eurasian level: Huitzilopochtli's slaying of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua at Coatepec is read by the Mexica-area-specialist tradition (Matos Moctezuma, Carrasco, López Austin) as a paradigmatic establishment-of-cosmic-order-and-sovereignty combat — the Mexica-state-political-ritual-centre version of the chaoskampf order-establishment pattern at full strength; the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the contrastive case at the opposite typological pole within the non-Eurasian comparison — the killing-as-hubris-and-hero-punished refusal held against the slaying-ESTABLISHES-sovereignty paradigm. The Coatepec-Mwindo contrast within the present book's non-Eurasian-cluster chapters is registered for Part II C7 and C8 as the principal worked instance of the *contrastive case within the non-Eurasian cluster* methodological move.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Pariacaca and the amaru (Ch. 26) — co-non-Eurasian-cluster chapter where the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing on grounds of an alternative load-bearing function (wak'a-succession in the Pariacaca case; maturation-through-humiliation in the Mwindo case); the Pariacaca cycle is the *transformation* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category transforms when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing) and the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the *contrastive-case-refusal* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category's surface combat-pattern present but tradition's own moral framing explicitly refuses the typology's order-establishing valence); the two chapters between them anchor the transformation and the refusal sub-problems of Part II C7 and C8 from different non-Eurasian-cluster locations within the present book — Pariacaca from the Andean cluster and Mwindo from the eastern central-African forest-zone Bantu cluster.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Bida and the founding of Wagadu (Ch. 28) — Africa-cluster co-comparandum and the present book's strongest "pattern doesn't go where comparativists expect" pairing per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels: the Bida narrative is the related-tier *inversion* case (slaying-ENDS-prosperity rather than establishing it; covenant-and-consequence framing) and the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the contrastive-case-tier *refusal* case (killing-as-hubris-and-hero-punished; maturation-through-humiliation framing) — with the Bida case operating at the level of the killing's *outcome* (the polity ends rather than is established) and the Mwindo case operating at the level of the killing's *moral evaluation within the narrative itself* (the killer is punished rather than celebrated, by an explicit named celestial authority). The Bida-Mwindo pairing within the Africa cluster is registered for Part II C7 and especially C8 as the principal worked instance of the *Africa-cluster post-killing-consequence-and-moral-refusal* methodological move and as the present book's clearest cluster-level "pattern doesn't go where comparativists expect" demonstration.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Nanabozho and Mishipeshu (Ch. 32) — co-contrastive-case-tier chapter on the Anishinaabe Hallowell-Johnston-T. Smith-Vizenor refusal of the combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing; the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu case is the refusal sub-problem in the strong sense of refusing the combat framing as such (the necessary-counterpart-dialectic reading replaces the combat reading entirely), while the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the refusal sub-problem in the form of refusing the chaoskampf typology's moral valence while retaining the surface combat-and-killing structural pattern (the maturation-through-humiliation reading frames the combat as transgression-and-correction within an already-existing cosmographic equilibrium). The Mwindo-Nanabozho pairing within the contrastive-case-tier classification is registered for Part II C8 as the principal worked instance of the *two distinct forms of refusal* methodological move (combat-framing refusal versus moral-valence refusal) within the wider refusal-and-appropriation problem.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the present chapter is a worked instance of three methodological moves: (1) the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement (scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3) applied to oral-tradition material recorded from a single named performer (Shé-Kárisi Rureke at Bese, 1956), with the chapter opening with the Nyanga tradition's maturation-through-humiliation framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category; (2) the contrastive-case-frame methodological move (the use of the comparativist contrastive-case-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation), which the present chapter establishes for the present book in its strongest form and which Part II C8 (Ch. 41) develops at full length; (3) the named-but-flagged secondary-specialists pattern for non-Eurasian non-textual traditions (Hale, Ngandu Nkashama, Mulokozi, Bird named in body prose alongside the Biebuyck-Mateene and Belcher anchors but not load-bearing for any individual EVIDENCE-anchored claim), per the Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 / Ch27 source-pinning / Ch28 FC-02 named-but-flagged precedents.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, Ch. 40) — secondary forward target: the present chapter contributes a contrastive-tier worked instance of the moral-valence-refusal sub-problem within the wider inversion / transformation problem complex, paired with the Bida case (Ch28) as the Africa-cluster co-comparandum and contrasted with the within-tradition Krishna-Kāliya transformation case (Ch15) and with the across-tradition Pariacaca-cycle transformation case (Ch26); the chapter's contrastive-case framing is to be held in C7 alongside the Bida-case inversion framing, with the methodological discipline that both frames are heuristics for cross-cultural reflection and that C7 is to investigate whether the frames ultimately obscure more than they reveal about the African-cluster traditions' own framings.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem, Ch. 41) — PRIMARY FORWARD TARGET. The present chapter is the BOOK'S CLEAREST WORKED INSTANCE of the refusal sub-problem proper (per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions): a story in which the chaoskampf category's surface combat-and-killing pattern is present but in which the tradition's own moral-and-cosmographic framing explicitly refuses the typology's order-establishing valence by punishing rather than celebrating the killer. To be paired in C8 with the Anishinaabe Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material (Ch. 32) as the structurally-distinct combat-framing-refusal co-case and with the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks) as the further methodological-critique case. The chapter's [INFERENCE] marker on the contrastive reading and [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker on the Nyanga-area-specialist objection are the principal markers that Part II C8 is to engage with directly.
```
